        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492"><meta property="og:title" content="Ice-Assisted Synthesis of Highly Crystallized Prussian Blue Analogues for All-Climate and Long-Calendar-Life Sodium Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/acs.nanolett.1c04492.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="For practical sodium-ion batteries, both high electrochemical performance and cost efficiency of the electrode materials are considered as two key parameters. Prussian blue analogues (PBAs) are broadly recognized as promising cathode materials due to their low cost, high theoretical capacity, and cycling stability, although they suffer from low-crystallinity-induced performance deterioration. Herein, a facile “ice-assisted” strategy is presented to prepare highly crystallized PBAs without any additives. By suppressing structure defects, the cathode exhibits a high capacity of 123 mAh g–1 with initial Coulombic efficiency of 87.2%, a long cycling lifespan of 3000 cycles, and significantly enhanced high/low temperature performance and calendar life. Remarkably, the low structure distortion and high sodium diffusion coefficient have been identified via in situ synchrotron powder diffraction and first-principles calculations, while its thermal stability has been analyzed by in situ heated X-ray powder diffraction. We believe the results could pave the way to the low-cost and large-scale application of PBAs in all-climate sodium-ion batteries."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/acs.nanolett.1c04492.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93187eb4f853075b-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187eb4f853075b-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'ce71ba73-c435-41e9-82fd-231b95f9ae16'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187eb4f853075b-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano_letters';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano_letters/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano_letters/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

data-references="https://pubs.acs.org/action/ajaxShowEnhancedAbstract?doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.1c04492&amp;ajax=true" data-enable-mathjax="true" vocab="http://schema.org/" typeof="ScholarlyArticle" class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a <script nonce="93187eb4f853075b-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "nalefd";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ice-Assisted Synthesis of Highly Crystallized Prussian Blue Analogues for All-Climate and Long-Calendar-Life Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ice-Assisted Synthesis of Highly Crystallized Prussian Blue Analogues for All-Climate and Long-Calendar-Life Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ice-Assisted Synthesis of Highly Crystallized Prussian Blue Analogues for All-Climate and Long-Calendar-Life Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ice-Assisted Synthesis of Highly Crystallized Prussian Blue Analogues for All-Climate and Long-Calendar-Life Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ice-Assisted Synthesis of Highly Crystallized Prussian Blue Analogues for All-Climate and Long-Calendar-Life Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ice-Assisted Synthesis of Highly Crystallized Prussian Blue Analogues for All-Climate and Long-Calendar-Life Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ice-Assisted Synthesis of Highly Crystallized Prussian Blue Analogues for All-Climate and Long-Calendar-Life Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ice-Assisted Synthesis of Highly Crystallized Prussian Blue Analogues for All-Climate and Long-Calendar-Life Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ice-Assisted Synthesis of Highly Crystallized Prussian Blue Analogues for All-Climate and Long-Calendar-Life Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Clicking on the donut icon will load a page at altmetric.com with additional details about the score and the social media presence for the given article. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/medium/nl1c04492_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/large/nl1c04492_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">For practical sodium-ion batteries, both high electrochemical performance and cost efficiency of the electrode materials are considered as two key parameters. Prussian blue analogues (PBAs) are broadly recognized as promising cathode materials due to their low cost, high theoretical capacity, and cycling stability, although they suffer from low-crystallinity-induced performance deterioration. Herein, a facile “ice-assisted” strategy is presented to prepare highly crystallized PBAs without any additives. By suppressing structure defects, the cathode exhibits a high capacity of 123 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with initial Coulombic efficiency of 87.2%, a long cycling lifespan of 3000 cycles, and significantly enhanced high/low temperature performance and calendar life. Remarkably, the low structure distortion and high sodium diffusion coefficient have been identified <i>via in situ</i> synchrotron powder diffraction and first-principles calculations, while its thermal stability has been analyzed by <i>in situ</i> heated X-ray powder diffraction. We believe the results could pave the way to the low-cost and large-scale application of PBAs in all-climate sodium-ion batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D1302%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DJian%2BPeng%252C%2BWang%2BZhang%252C%2BZhe%2BHu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D22%26issueNum%3D3%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.1c04492%26title%3DIce-Assisted%2BSynthesis%2Bof%2BHighly%2BCrystallized%2BPrussian%2BBlue%2BAnalogues%2Bfor%2BAll-Climate%2Band%2BLong-Calendar-Life%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D1310%26publicationDate%3DFebruary%2B2022" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2022 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291880&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Crystal structure"> Crystal structure </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291484&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffraction"> Diffraction </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties"> Electrical properties </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">

                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Ice-assisted+Synthesis&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ice-assisted%20synthesis">ice-assisted synthesis</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=High+Crystallinity&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_high%20crystallinity">high crystallinity</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Low+Defect&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_low%20defect">low defect</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Prussian+Blue+Analogue&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Prussian%20blue%20analogue">Prussian blue analogue</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20battery">sodium-ion battery</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The past decades have witnessed rapidly increasing concerns about the sustainability of fossil fuels, which leads to an urgent demand for renewable energy sources to meet the energy needs of human society and development. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> Grid-scale energy storage systems (GESS) are vital to bridge the disconnect between the intermittent renewable energy supply and fluctuating consumption. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 3 and 4">(3,4)</a> Although advanced lithium-ion batteries have achieved high market shares in commercial portable devices and electric vehicles, the limited lithium resources call for an alternative, especially for application in GESS. Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are generally regarded as more promising candidates than their lithium counterparts for GESS due to their low cost, high abundance, and intrinsic safety. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 5 and 6">(5,6)</a> To date, tremendous efforts are still needed for developing high-energy-density and long-lifespan electrode materials, especially for the cathodes, to achieve competitive performance of SIBs, based on optimizing the structures of materials and achieving a comprehensive in-depth understanding of Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanisms. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 7 to 9">(7−9)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Recently, various types of cathodes have been reported for SIBs, including transition-metal layered oxides, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 10 to 12">(10−12)</a> polyanions, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13,14)</a> and Prussian blue analogues (PBAs). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 15 to 17">(15−17)</a> Among these reported cathode materials, PBAs with the general chemical formula of A<sub><i>x</i></sub>P[R(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub><i>y</i></sub>·<i>n</i>H<sub>2</sub>O·□<sub><i>z</i></sub> (A: alkali metals, P/R: transition metal cations, □: [R(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies) are regarded as one class of promising candidates and have been widely studied because of their high theoretical capacity, facile synthetic procedure, low cost, and nontoxicity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18,19)</a> The large interstices and cation diffusion channels inside the framework of PBAs allow rapid cation intercalation without obvious distortion, which also makes PBAs desirable cathodes for fast-charging batteries. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 20 to 22">(20−22)</a> However, with these attractive features, the practical application of PBAs in SIBs has been hindered by the 3-fold challenges caused by the large amount of defects, as we discussed previously. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> The Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>4–/3–</sup> vacancies are usually caused by the fast chemical precipitation kinetics and degrade the electrochemical performance of PBAs in terms of specific capacity, cycle life, rate performance, etc. Therefore, the crystallization control of PBAs is confirmed to be an effective route for the preparation of high-performance PBA-based SIBs and typically achieved by the following three routes: (1) Chelates, such as sodium citrate, ethylenediaminetetraacetic acid (EDTA), sodium pyrophosphate, etc., are widely applied to suppress the growth rate of the nuclei and prevent serious grain aggregation, so as to obtain well-crystallized PBAs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 23 to 26">(23−26)</a> (2) By using the decomposition reaction of a hexacyanometallate salt in an acid solution, high-quality PBAs could be obtained by the coprecipitation between the hexacyanometallate salt and the slowly released transition metal cations. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 27 to 29">(27−29)</a> (3) Additives, including surfactants and sodium salts, have been reported that could be beneficial to the crystal growth process and result in sodium-rich well-grown PBAs with enhanced electrochemical performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 30 to 32">(30−32)</a> These strategies are generally limited, however, either by their high cost, fatal-size byproduct (cyanide), or impurities, which further hinder their application in the large-scale production of PBAs. Therefore, the design of new synthesis methods to prepare well-crystallized PBAs is of vital importance.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, we present a one-step “ice-assisted” synthesis method to prepare highly crystallized PBAs without any additives. Benefiting from the slow precipitation rate achieved by slowly thawing the raw materials, well-crystallized PBA nanoparticles are obtained and exhibit excellent electrochemical performance as cathode materials for SIBs with long calendar lifespan and all-climate capability (−10 to 60 °C). We reveal detailed structure–function relationships and the sodium-ion storage mechanisms of PBAs systematically through advanced <i>in situ</i> techniques together with theoretical calculations. The results indicate that these additive-free PBAs are promising cathode candidates for next-generation GESS.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">First, two kinds of Na<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub><i>y</i></sub> were synthesized via the “ice-assisted” two-phase method and the typical water-based coprecipitation method (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/suppl_file/nl1c04492_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Experimental Section</a> in detail and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/suppl_file/nl1c04492_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> in the Supporting Information), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> samples of which were denoted as “I-PB” and “W-PB”, respectively. Synchrotron powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) with Rietveld refinement was employed on GSAS software to investigate their crystal structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 1</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/suppl_file/nl1c04492_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S2</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35 ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 34 to 36">(34−36)</a> As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a, the sharp PXRD peaks demonstrate the well-crystallized cubic phase (space group: <i>Fm</i>3̅<i>m</i>) of I-PB without any impurities. The lattice parameters of I-PB slightly decreased from 10.28 to 10.09 Å compared with that of W-PB (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/suppl_file/nl1c04492_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Tables S1 and S2</a> in the Supporting Information), an indication of a suppressed amount of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> defects. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> The corresponding crystal structure of I-PB (inset in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a) shows that the low-spin Fe ions (green octahedra) are coordinated to the C of C≡N<sup>–</sup> with the high-spin Fe (brown octahedra) bonded with N, forming an open three-dimensional (3D) structure with large channels for fast ion transport. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref37" aria-label="reference 15 and 37">(15,37)</a> The thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) demonstrates that the adsorbed water and interstitial water content (weight loss from room temperature to 600 °C) of I-PB is lower than that of W-PB prepared by the typical water-based coprecipitation method (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b). Combining inductively coupled plasma–optical emission spectroscopy (ICP-OES) and element analysis, the chemical formulas of I-PB and W-PB can be represented as Na<sub>1.58</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.87</sub>·2.38H<sub>2</sub>O and Na<sub>1.12</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.72</sub>·5.25H<sub>2</sub>O, respectively. The content of the Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> defect in I-PB is significantly suppressed from 28% to 13% due to the low crystal growth rate. Furthermore, the higher sodium-ion content and less coordinated water could further promote the well-crystallized structure of I-PB obtained from the ice-assisted low-speed precipitation process. The I-PB sample also shows high storage stability when exposed to air for 43 days, which is ascribed to the stable framework with low defect, while tiny impurities were formed for W-PB (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 1</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/suppl_file/nl1c04492_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S3</a>). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/suppl_file/nl1c04492_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a> shows the Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FT-IR) spectra of the samples. The peaks at ∼493, ∼1610, and ∼3447 cm<sup>–1</sup> are assigned to the vibrations of O–H and H–O–H bending modes arising from the free surface water and the interstitial water, respectively, while the peaks at ∼600 and ∼2081 cm<sup>–1</sup> are associated with the vibrations of the cyanide groups in [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>3–/4–</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The FTIR results indicate the existence of the water molecules inside and on the surface of both as-prepared samples. The vibration state of C≡N<sup>–</sup> is highly sensitive to the valence state of Fe, so Raman spectroscopy was employed to show the valence state of the Fe (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/suppl_file/nl1c04492_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a</a>). The strong peak at ∼2147 cm<sup>–1</sup> and the weak peak at ∼2090 cm<sup>–1</sup> indicate the existence of a mixture of Fe<sup>2+/3+</sup> coordinated with nitrogen, which means part of the Fe<sup>2+</sup> was oxidized into Fe<sup>3+</sup>. The existence of Fe<sup>3+</sup> might be ascribed to the inevitable oxidation of the PBAs when exposed to air (O<sub>2</sub>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> The BET specific surface areas (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/suppl_file/nl1c04492_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5b</a>) of I-PB and W-PB are 49.8 and 38.7 m<sup>2</sup>/g, respectively. The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) images indicate that both I-PB and W-PB have a uniform ball-shaped morphology with a particle diameter of ∼100 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 1</a>d–f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/suppl_file/nl1c04492_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S6–S9</a>). The STEM-based energy dispersive spectrometer images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>g) confirm the even distribution of Na, Fe, C, N, and O, which further indicates the intact crystal structure and high purity of I-PB.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/medium/nl1c04492_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/large/nl1c04492_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Rietveld refinement of I-PB with inserted schematic representation of the structure of I-PB. The green octahedra and brown octahedra represent FeC<sub>6</sub> and FeN<sub>6</sub> groups, respectively, and the orange balls indicate Na<sup>+</sup> ions. (b) TGA analysis of I-PB and W-PB from room temperature to 600 °C. (c) X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of I-PB before and after the storage test in air. (d) SEM image, (e, f) transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images, and (g) STEM–energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping results for detected elements C, N, Na, and Fe.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/large/nl1c04492_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The sodium-ion storage performances of W-PB and I-PB electrodes are compared in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a,d. W-PB prepared by a typical coprecipitation method could only deliver 91 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the current density of 15 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 57.7%. In comparison to W-PB, I-PB shows a better sodium-ion storage performance that reaches a reversible discharge specific capacity of 123 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a high ICE of 87.2%. The enhanced ICE and sodium-ion storage sites are mainly due to the benefits of the decreased amounts of coordinated water and [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>3-/4–</sup> vacancies, leading to the suppression of side reactions between the electrode and the electrolyte. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40,41)</a> To investigate the rate performance of I-PB, the galvanostatic charge/discharge curves recorded at the current densities of 30, 75, 150, 300, 750, and 1500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> show the reversible discharge capacities of 117, 109, 104, 98, 91, and 85 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, with Coulombic efficiency near 100%, which is much higher than the values for W-PB at the corresponding current densities, especially under high currents (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b,e,f). This means I-PB could be fully charged/discharged in just ∼3.5 min, but it still retains ∼75% of its specific capacity at 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. It is notable that the capacity could recover to 98.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> when the current density decreases to 75 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (corresponding to the capacity retention of ∼90%), indicating that the crystal structure could remain almost stable even during fast ion shuttling. The capacity decay may be due to the loss of the reversible sodium ion storage sites at the Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancy position and partly to the crystal structure collapse during the cycling process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref42" aria-label="reference 16 and 42">(16,42)</a> The cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the initial 5 cycles at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c, where there are two distinct redox pairs at 3.05/2.68 V and 3.59/3.22 V, which could be assigned to the low-spin Fe (C) couples and high-spin Fe (N) couples, respectively. The weak peak at 3.32 V might be due to a small amount of Na<sup>+</sup> extracted from the 24d (edge position of the cubic structure). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> The weak peaks of the oxidation process at ∼4.0 V, which gradually disappeared, exhibit the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a> Furthermore, W-PB showed poor cycling stability, given that the specific discharge capacity decreased to ∼50 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 500 cycles at 150 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, while I-PB could retain ∼90 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>g). More importantly, I-PB shows a higher capacity retention of 64.9% over ∼3000 cycles at a high current density of 750 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with Coulombic efficiency (CE) of ∼100% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>h), corresponding to a low capacity fading of 0.01% per cycle. The charge/discharge curves are highly repeatable, and the peaks for the redox couples remain similar to those in the initial curves (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/suppl_file/nl1c04492_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>), which further demonstrates the structural stability and the high reversibility of I-PB.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/medium/nl1c04492_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/large/nl1c04492_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Charge and discharge profiles of (a, b) W-PB and (d, e) I-PB for the first five cycles and with different current densities. (c) Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of I-PB and W-PB (inset). (f) Rate performances of W-PB and I-PB at current densities from 30 to 1500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (g, h) Cycling performances of W-PB and I-PB at the current densities of 150 and 750 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/large/nl1c04492_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further investigate the sodium storage mechanism of I-PB, synchrotron <i>in situ</i> PXRD was employed to analyze the phase transitions during the sodium-ion insertion and extraction processes. The homemade <i>in situ</i> PXRD cell was fully charged to 4.2 V and discharged to 2.0 V for two cycles to form a stable electrode interface before carrying out the test. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a, the corresponding two-dimensional (2D) contour patterns of the reflections from the (200), (220), (400), and (420) planes were selected for specific investigation. A three-phase transition process was observed as we reported previously. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> From 2.0 to 3.3 V, all peaks shift gradually to higher angles due to volume shrinkage caused by the sodium-ion extraction. During this process, the phase transition from rhombohedral to cubic was observed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b), which contributes nearly half of the capacity with the redox reaction of high-spin Fe<sup>2+</sup> to Fe<sup>3+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> During the subsequent charging process to 4.2 V, the peaks start shifting toward lower angles, indicating the structural evolution from cubic (∼1 Na<sup>+</sup> storage per unit cell) to tetragonal phase (without Na<sup>+</sup> inside the crystal structure), which can be ascribed to the sodium-ion extraction from the crystal structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> The background peak located at ∼19.2° remains stable in both position and intensity, demonstrating that the above-mentioned peak variations are caused by structure evolution of the electrode materials. During the discharge process, all peaks of I-PB gradually shift in the reverse direction to their initial positions and intensities, corresponding to the phase transition from tetragonal to cubic followed by the cubic to rhombohedral phase transition again. The highly reversible phase evolution during the charge and discharge processes is schematically illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/medium/nl1c04492_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/large/nl1c04492_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) 2D contour plots of (200), (220), (400), and (420) reflection planes with corresponding charge and discharge curves obtained from synchrotron <i>in situ</i> PXRD of I-PB. (b) Schematic illustration of the phase transition mechanisms of I-PB during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/large/nl1c04492_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><i>In situ</i> heated PXRD was conducted to investigate the dehydration behavior and thermal stability of W-PB and I-PB from room temperature to 400 °C, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a–e. Both samples exhibit the cubic structure at room temperature, but W-PB gradually transforms to the trigonal phase from ∼160 °C and completes this phase transition at ∼200 °C. In comparison, I-PB shows higher thermal stability and could maintain its cubic structure up to ∼260 °C. This indicates that the thermal stability could be enhanced through improving the crystallization, which would be of benefit for the fabrication of SIBs that could be applied over a wide temperature range.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/medium/nl1c04492_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/large/nl1c04492_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. <i>In situ</i> heated XRD patterns of (a, b) I-PB and (c, d) W-PB. (e) Phase evolution of I-PB and W-PB during the heating treatment. (f) Two possible Na<sup>+</sup> migration paths inside the defect-rich crystal structure of W-PB and the defect-free crystal structure of I-PB (green ball: Fe; gray ball: C; yellow ball: N; purple ball: Na). (g) Corresponding migration energies of the two paths in W-PB and I-PB.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/large/nl1c04492_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were employed to further investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> storage and migration mechanisms. Taking the symmetry of the cubic structure into consideration, there are three kinds of possible host interstices for guest ions, which could be denoted as 8c, 24d, and 32f according to Wyckoff notation (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/suppl_file/nl1c04492_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>). Among the three sites, 24d offers the smallest free space and the 8c could offer the largest, while the defect-rich crystal structure would form large interstices due to the loss of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> octahedra, which are typically occupied by the coordinated water molecules. As we previously reported, the Na<sup>+</sup> could be inserted into the defect-rich crystal structure at the interstices occupied by the water molecules, but it would be hard to extract from the water cluster, leading to the low specific capacity when this defect-rich W-PB is applied as the cathode material for SIBs. The effect of the intact crystal structure was also studied. Two possible Na<sup>+</sup> migration paths were considered to investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> transfer mechanism in two different crystal structures: (Path 1) Na<sup>+</sup> is transferred along the axis through the defect/unit cell center and (Path 2) Na<sup>+</sup> migrates along an S-shaped route bypassing the nearest defect/unit center (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>g shows the corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> migration energy barrier, where Path 2 exhibits the lower energy barrier in both kinds of crystal structures, indicating that the S-shape path is preferred for Na<sup>+</sup> migration. Remarkably, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier inside the intact crystal structure (0.23 eV for Path 1 and 0.11 eV for Path 2) is much lower than for the defect-rich crystal structure (2.63 eV for Path 1 and 2.81 eV for Path 2), leading to fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>g). Therefore, increasing the crystallinity is favorable for the fabrication of PBA electrode materials with high rate performance for SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">We further tested the storage and wide-temperature performance of I-PB and W-PB to provide more practical details relevant to their practical application. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> The all-climate storage performance of batteries is very important (especially for the batteries applied in GESS), but it has received limited attention. Therefore, investigation of the storage performance was carried out at different temperatures, ranging from −10 to 60 °C, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a–c. The batteries were fully charged to 4.2 V and transferred to a temperature-controlled chamber after charging and discharging for 5 cycles each week to fabricate a stable cathode–electrolyte interphase (CEI) at the current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> I-PB showed improved charge storage performance over a wide temperature range (−10 °C, 25 °C, 60 °C) compared with defect-rich W-PB, which may due to the enhanced thermal stability and suppressed water content of the crystal structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> After being stored for 1 week, the specific capacity retentions of I-PB remained 93.2%, 86.5%, and 66.4% at −10 °C, 25 °C, and 60 °C, respectively. As a comparison, W-PB could only maintain the capacity retentions of 82.3%, 80.1%, and 52.2% at the corresponding temperatures, indicating that electricity loss could be suppressed through improving the crystallization (with less water content inside the crystal structure) of the electrode by the proposed “ice-assisted” method. More significantly, I-PB gradually showed better capacity retention in the following weeks, while the charge loss ratio was also suppressed and remained stable from the third week, which may due to the formation of the stable cathode–electrolyte interface (CEI). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/medium/nl1c04492_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/large/nl1c04492_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a–c) Capacity retention ratios of I-PB and W-PB after storage at −10 °C, 25 °C, and 60 °C, respectively. (d, g) Rate performance at current densities from 30 to 1500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, (e, h) long-term cycling performance at a current density of 150 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, and (f, i) energy efficiency at current densities from 30 to 1500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> of I-PB and W-PB at all-climate conditions (blue: −10 °C; yellow: 60 °C).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/large/nl1c04492_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">In addition, we also evaluated the electrochemical performance of I-PB and W-PB over the wide temperature range from −10 to 60 °C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a>d–i and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/suppl_file/nl1c04492_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S12</a>). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d, the specific capacity of I-PB could reach 110.2 and 98.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under −10 °C at 30 and 75 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, but fast capacity fading was encountered with increasing current density. In addition, the I-PB electrode exhibits a superior low temperature cycling stability with ∼100% capacity retention at 150 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles, as well as a high specific capacity of 93.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and average CE of ∼100% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e). Due to the large amounts of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies and crystal water inside the crystal structure, W-PB only showed around half the specific capacity of I-PB at the corresponding current densities (66.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and 54.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 75 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) and lower energy efficiency. Subsequently, the specific capacity of I-PB recovered to 132.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup> as the temperature was increased to 60 °C and showed a high specific capacity of 90.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> even at 1500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to ∼72.6% capacity retention at 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g,h). Significantly, both the CE and the energy efficiency could reach ∼100% and ∼95% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f,i, respectively. This indicates that I-PB can be regarded as a promising candidate as a cathode material for practical SIBs, regarding both the electrochemical parameters and the kinetics factors.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">We have successfully designed an additive-free “ice-assisted” method to prepare highly crystallized I-PB with a highly reversible structure, sufficient Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites, and fast cation migration channels due to the suppressed concentration of defects. As a result, I-PB shows a high specific capacity of 123 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and an excellent cycling life up to 3000 cycles. More importantly, the removal of the defects plays an important role in the enhanced thermal stability of I-PB and makes it suitable as a cathode for all-climate SIBs (−10 to 60 °C). The <i>in situ</i> synchrotron PXRD results revealed the highly reversible structural evolution process and negligible volume distortion, while the DFT calculations verified the impressive low energy barriers to sodium ion diffusion and verified the cation storage mechanism in detail. Our studies suggest that this all-climate applicable, long-calendar-life, and low-cost I-PB material is a competitive candidate among the various cathode materials suitable for the practical application of SIBs in large-scale GESS in the near future.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Photograph of the experimental process for producing I-PB; synchrotron powder diffraction pattern of W-PB; XRD patterns of fresh W-PB and W-PB after aging in air for 43 days; FT-IR and Raman spectra of the as-prepared samples; SEM images of I-PB and W-PB; charge and discharge curves of W-PB and I-PB at the current densities of 150 and 750 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; low temperature (−10 °C) charge and discharge curves of W-PB and I-PB; and detailed structural information on the I-PB and W-PB samples after Rietveld refinement (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/suppl_file/nl1c04492_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/suppl_file/nl1c04492_si_001.pdf">nl1c04492_si_001.pdf (1.91 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Estimation of global final-stage energy-return-on-investment for fossil fuels with comparison to renewable energy sources</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Brockway, Paul E.; Owen, Anne; Brand-Correa, Lina I.; Hardt, Lukas</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">612-621</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2058-7546</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Research</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Under many scenarios, fossil fuels are projected to remain the dominant energy source until at least 2050.  However, harder-to-reach fossil fuels require more energy to ext. and, hence, are coming at an increasing 'energy cost'.  Assocd. declines in fossil fuel energy-return-on-investment ratios at first appear of little concern, given that published ests. for oil, coal and gas are typically above 25:1.  However, such ratios are measured at the primary energy stage and should instead be estd. at the final stage where energy enters the economy (for example, electricity and petrol).  Here, we calc. global time series (1995-2011) energy-return-on-investment ratios for fossil fuels at both primary and final energy stages.  We concur with common primary-stage ests. (∼30:1), but find very low ratios at the final stage: around 6:1 and declining.  This implies that fossil fuel energy-return-on-investment ratios may be much closer to those of renewables than previously expected and that they could decline precipitously in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1038/451652a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Armand, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J.-M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Building better batteries</span>. <i>Nature</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2008</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>451</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">7179</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">652</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">657</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202100757"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hua, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lai, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, W.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Architecting Amorphous Vanadium Oxide/MXene Nanohybrid via Tunable Anodic Oxidation for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv. Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>11</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">22</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">2100757</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.202100757</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D11%26publication_year%3D2021%26pages%3D2100757%26journal%3DAdv.%2BEnergy%2BMater.%26issue%3D22%26author%3DW.%2BZhang%26author%3DJ.%2BPeng%26author%3DW.%2BHua%26author%3DY.%2BLiu%26author%3DJ.%2BWang%26author%3DY.%2BLiang%26author%3DW.%2BLai%26author%3DY.%2BJiang%26author%3DY.%2BHuang%26author%3DW.%2BZhang%26title%3DArchitecting%2BAmorphous%2BVanadium%2BOxide%252FMXene%2BNanohybrid%2Bvia%2BTunable%2BAnodic%2BOxidation%2Bfor%2BHigh-Performance%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Faenm.202100757&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.1c04492&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Faenm.202100757&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Architecting Amorphous Vanadium Oxide/MXene Nanohybrid via Tunable Anodic Oxidation for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Wang; Peng, Jian; Hua, Weibo; Liu, Ying; Wang, Jinsong; Liang, Yaru; Lai, Weihong; Jiang, Yue; Huang, Yang; Zhang, Wei; Yang, Huiling; Yang, Yingguo; Li, Lina; Liu, Zhenjie; Wang, Lei; Chou, Shu-Lei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">22</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2100757</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Structural engineering and creating at. disorder in electrodes are promising strategies for highly efficient and rapid charge storage in advanced batteries.  Herein, a nanohybrid architecture is presented with amorphous vanadium oxide conformally coated on layered V2C MXene (a-VOx/V2C) via tunable anodic oxidn., which exhibits a high reversible capacity of 307 mAh g-1 at 50 mA g-1, decent rate capability with capacity up to 96 mAh g-1 at 2000 mA g-1, and good cycling stability as a cathode for sodium-ion batteries.  The a-VOx layer enables reversible and fast Na+ insertion/extn. by providing sufficient vacancies and open pathways in the amorphous framework, unlike the irreversible phase transition in its cryst. counterpart, while layered V2C MXene offers abundant electron/ion transfer channels, which are joined together to boost the electrochem. performance.  Notably the improved reversibility and structural superiority of the a-VOx/V2C nanohybrid are clearly revealed by in situ Raman, in situ transmission electron microscopy, in situ synchrotron X-ray absorption spectroscopy, and d. functional theory calcns., demonstrating a reversible V-O vibration and valence oscillation between V4+ andV5+ in the disordered framework, with robust structural stability and unobstructed Na+ diffusion.  This work provides a meaningful ref. for the elaborate design of MXene-based nanostructured electrodes toward advanced rechargeable batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1038/natrevmats.2018.13"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vaalma, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Buchholz, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Weil, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A cost and resource analysis of sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Nat.
