        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The exploration of next-generation sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) is a worldwide concern to replace the current com. lithium-ion batteries, mitigating the increasing exhaustion of Li resources.  Sodium transition metal oxides are considered to be one of the most promising cathode materials for SIBs.  The anionic redox reaction in Li-rich transition metal oxides is capable of providing extra capacity in addn. to the cationic redox activities in lithium-ion batteries.  A similar phenomenon exists in SIBs, which even applies to Na-deficient transition metal oxides.  Moreover, transition metal oxides with mixed phase also demonstrate great potential.  In this review, studies on anionic redox are first systematically introduced.  The up-to-date advances on high-capacity transition metal oxide cathode materials for SIBs are then classified and summarized in different groups assocd. with or without anionic redox.  The existing challenges as well as available solns. and strategies are discussed, and proposals with new insights are made at the end.  It is expected that this work can provide new perspectives on controlling the anionic redox activity and finding novel high-capacity oxide cathode materials for SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-021-22523-3"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, G.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, Y.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, R.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Tuning local chemistry of P2 layered-oxide cathode for high energy and long cycles of sodium-ion battery</span>. <i>Nat. However, they are hindered by the limited capacity and inferior phase transition due to the gliding of transition-metal layers upon Na+ extn. and insertion in the cathode materials.  Here, we report that the large-sized K+ is riveted in the prismatic Na+ sites of P2-Na0.612K0.056MnO2 to enable more thermodynamically favorable Na+ vacancies. However, the layered cathode tends to be thermodynamically and electrochem. unstable during (de)sodiation.  Herein, we propose the pinning effect and controllable pinning point in sodium storage layered cathodes to enhance the structural stability and achieve optimal electrochem. performance. 0%, 2.5% and 7.3% transition-metal occupancies in Na-site as pinning points are obtained in Na0.67Mn0.5Co0.5-xFexO2. 2.5% Na-site pinned by Fe3+ is beneficial to restrain the potential slab sliding and enhance the structural stability, resulting in an ultra-low vol. variation of 0.6% and maintaining the smooth two-dimensional channel for Na-ion transfer.  The Na0.67Mn0.5Co0.4Fe0.1O2 cathode with the optimal Fe3+ pinning delivers outstanding cycle performance of over 1000 cycles and superior rate capability up to 10 C.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref13" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-021-23132-w"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qi, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.-S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Rapid mechanochemical synthesis of polyanionic cathode with improved electrochemical performance for Na-ion batteries</span>. <i>Nat.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Stabilizing fluorine to achieve high-voltage and ultra-stable Na3V2(PO4)2F3 cathode for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Deng, Liang; Yu, Fu-Da; Xia, Yang; Jiang, Yun-Shan; Sui, Xu-Lei; Zhao, Lei; Meng, Xiang-Hui; Que, Lan-Fang; Wang, Zhen-Bo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na3V2(PO4)2F3 is a promising cathode candidate for sodium ion batteries due to its high working voltage.  However, an "unknown" low-voltage plateau around ∼3.3 V lowers its operating voltage and puzzles researchers.  Herein, the reasons for its appearance are explored.  It is found that the formation of the low-voltage plateau originates from the extra-generated [VO6] induced by the fluorine loss during the synthesis.  To enable better performance, a feasible strategy of stabilizing fluorine is proposed to eliminate the low-voltage plateau.  It is demonstrated that this strategy can effectively guarantee the existence of fluorine and modulate the local electronic structure of V to maintain [VO4F2] in Na3V2(PO4)2F3 phase, thus eliminating the undesirable plateau and achieving an operating-voltage increase of ∼100 mV.  Moreover, the enhanced structural stability and kinetics of the optimized Na3V2(PO4)2F3 cathode are confirmed by in-situ XRD.  As expected, the Na3V2(PO4)2F3 cathode delivers higher energy d. (446.4 Wh kg-1), better rate capability and longer cycling performance (89.2%@30 C after 1000 cycles).  Furthermore, its excellent adaptability to wide temp. range (-25 °C-55 °C) and strong competence in Na3V2(PO4)2F3||hard carbon full-cell are verified.  These excellent properties further qualifyNa3V2(PO4)2F3 as a competitive cathode for sodium ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202000943"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xue, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hexacyanoferrate-type Prussian blue analogs: principles and advances toward high-performance sodium and potassium ion batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hexacyanoferrate-Type Prussian Blue Analogs: Principles and Advances Toward High-Performance Sodium and Potassium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhou, Aijun; Cheng, Weijie; Wang, Wei; Zhao, Qiang; Xie, Jian; Zhang, Wuxing; Gao, Hongcai; Xue, Leigang; Li, Jingze</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>

Na-ion batteries (NIBs) and K-ion batteries (KIBs) are promising candidates for next-generation elec. energy storage applications due to their low costs and appreciable energy/power d. compared to Li-ion batteries.  In the search for viable electrode materials for NIBs and KIBs, Prussian blue analogs (PBAs) with inherent rigid and open frameworks and large interstitial voids have shown an impressive ability to accommodate big alkali-metal ions without structure collapse.  In particular, hexacyanoferrates (HCFs) utilizing abundant Fe(CN)6 resources are the most interesting subgroup of PBAs, being able to deliver a specific capacity of 70-170 mAh g-1 and a voltage of 2.5-3.8 V in NIBs/KIBs.  In this Review, a comprehensive discussion of the HCF-type cathode materials in terms of their structural features, redox mechanisms, synthesis control, and modification strategies based on research advances over the last ten years.  The methodologies and achievements in improving the material properties of HCFs including the compositional stoichiometry, crystal water, crystallinity, morphol., and elec. cond. are outlined, with the aim to promote understanding of these materials and provide new insights into future design of PBAs for advanced rechargeable batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1039/D1EE00087J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ou, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yi, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">López, C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">For reaching high-performance of electrode materials, it is generally believed that understanding the structure evolution and heterogeneous alignment effect is the key.  Presently, a very simple and universally applicable self-healing method is investigated to prep. defect-free Prussian blue analogs (PBAs) that reach their theor. capacity as cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  For direct imaging of the local structure and the dynamic process at the at. scale, we deliver a fast ion-conductive nickel-based PBA that enables rapid Na+ extn./insertion within 3 min and a capacity retention of nearly 100% over 4000 cycles. Unconventional materials and mechanisms that enable reversible insertion/extn. of ions in low-cost metal-org. frameworks (MOFs) within minutes have implications for fast-charging devices, grid-scale energy storage applications, material discovery, and tailored modification.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1002/cssc.201902013"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qing, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">In Situ Self-Assembly of Core-Shell Multimetal Prussian Blue Analogues for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ChemSusChem</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">21</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">4786</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">4790</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Metal-org. frameworks (MOFs), such as Prussian blue and its analogs (PB and PBAs) with open frameworks have attracted tremendous attentions as cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries, owing to their simple method of synthesis and high theor. specific capacity.  In this study, core-shell-structured PBAs are prepd. by an in situ self-assembly method.  Owing to the advantages of both constituents, the as-prepd. core-shell PBAs show excellent rate and cycling electrochem. properties through a dual-level-controlled charge-discharge depth mechanism.  It delivers a specific capacity of 104.3 mAh g-1 at 0.1 C, as well as a remarkably enhanced cycle performance, giving 88.3% of its initial capacity over 1000 cycles at 300 mA g-1.  In particular, the coating strategy described herein could be extended to other MOF materials, leading to wider application in energy storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1016/j.mtener.2021.100898"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ye, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">You, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiong, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, C.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In-situ construction of a NaF-rich cathode-electrolyte interface on Prussian blue toward a 3000-cycle-life sodium-ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ye, Minghui; You, Shunzhang; Xiong, Jiaming; Yang, Yang; Zhang, Yufei; Li, Cheng Chao</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Materials Today Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">23</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">100898</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">MTEACH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2468-6069</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">As a kind of high-voltage cathode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), the instability of a cathode-electrolyte interface (CEI) of Prussian blue (PB) results in the consumption of electrolyte and poor cycling life.  Herein, an artificial NaF-rich CEI is in-situ designed on a PB cathode via a chem. presodiated strategy to remarkably prolong the cycling life of SIBs.  Based on a spontaneous redox reaction between a chem. presodiation solvent and a polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) binder, a homogeneous, thin, and NaF-rich CEI is conformally coated on the surface of PB, forming CEI@PB.  The components of CEI within CEI@PB mainly comprise inorg. Na-salts (NaF, Na2CO3, and NaHCO3) and org. Na-based species (R-O-Na, R-CO-Na, and R-O2CO-Na), which can ensure the rapid transport of Na+ ions while effectively preventing CEI@PB against the attack of org. solvent and improving its cycling stability.  Besides, d. functional theory calcns. also reveal that NaF is a good electronic insulator and ionic conductor, which is suitable as a stable CEI.  Benefiting from the aforementioned merits, the CEI@PB cathode with a high areal capacity of 0.61 mAh cm-2 delivers an ultra-long cycling life of 3000 cycles.  More importantly, the concept of in-situ CEI can be extended to other cathodes beyond Na batteries discussed herein.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref19" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.202108616"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, X.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lai, W.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.-L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Advanced Characterization Techniques Paving the Way for Commercialization of Low-Cost Prussian Blue Analog Cathodes</span>. <i>Adv. Metal-org. frameworks (MOFs) and their derivs. have spurred extensive exploration as they provide a library of new electrode materials.  The rich and structural flexibilities (such as metal nodes, ligands, pore structure) endow MOFs and MOFs-derivs. with vast opportunities for various energy devices.                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium storage in Na-rich NaxFeFe(CN)6 nanocubes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Yang; Qiao, Yun; Zhang, Wuxing; Li, Zhen; Ji, Xiao; Miao, Ling; Yuan, Lixia; Hu, Xianluo; Huang, Yunhui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-rich prussian blue analogs (PBs) with high coulombic efficiency are of fundamental and technol. importance for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  Here we report high-quality NaxFeFe(CN)6 nanocubes as cathode materials for SIBs and investigate their sodium storage mechanism.  Among them, Na-rich Na1.70FeFe(CN)6 shows highly reversible electrochem. reactions, delivering a capacity as high as 120.7 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 200 mA g-1; even at 1200 mA g-1, the capacity still retains up to 73.6 mA h g-1.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Prussian blue and its analogs have received particular attention as superior cathodes for Na-ion batteries due to their potential 2-Na storage capacity (∼170 mA-h g-1) and low cost.  However, most of the Prussian blue compds. obtained from the conventional synthetic routes contain large amts. of Fe(CN)6 vacancies and coordinated H2O mols., which leads to the collapse of cyano-bridged framework and serious deterioration of their Na-storage ability.  Herein, the authors propose a facile citrate-assisted controlled crystn. method to obtain low-defect Prussian blue lattice with greatly improved Na-storage capacity and cycling stability.  As an example, the as-prepd. Na2CoFe(CN)6 nanocrystals demonstrate a reversible 2-Na storage reaction with a high specific capacity of 150 mA-h g-1 and a ∼ 90% capacity retention over 200 cycles, possibly serving as a low cost and high performance cathode for Na-ion batteries.  In particular, the synthetic strategy described in this work may be extended to other coordination-framework materials for a wide range of energy conversion and storage applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201803158"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wan, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ou, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, X.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Structure Distortion Induced Monoclinic Nickel Hexacyanoferrate as High-Performance Cathode for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The design of Faradaic battery electrodes that exhibit high rate capability and long cycle life equiv. to those of the electrodes of elec. double-layer capacitors is a big challenge.  Here we report a strategy to fill this performance gap using the concept of Grotthuss proton conduction, in which proton transfer takes place by means of concerted cleavage and formation of O-H bonds in a hydrogen-bonding network.  We show that in a hydrated Prussian blue analog (Turnbull's blue) the abundant lattice water mols. with a contiguous hydrogen-bonding network facilitate Grotthuss proton conduction during redox reactions.  When using it as a battery electrode, we find high-rate behaviors at 4,000 C (380 A g-1, 508 mA cm-2), and a long cycling life of 0.73 million cycles.  These results for diffusion-free Grotthuss topochem. of protons, in contrast to orthodox battery electrochem., which requires ion diffusion inside electrodes, indicate a potential direction to revolutionize electrochem. energy storage for high-power applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref38" class="refNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit38" data-doi="10.3390/cryst11080895"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Morozova, P.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Microbial fuel cells can directly convert chem. energy into elec. energy, but significant energy losses result from the use of O2 as the cathode.  Microbial batteries (MBs) replace conventional O2 cathodes with solid-state cathodes that can be re-oxidized under favorable conditions, enabling more efficient energy recovery.  Previously, we demonstrated proof-of-concept for MBs with a silver oxide cathode.  The high cost of silver and energy required for re-oxidn. make this material impractical.  In this work, we evaluate Prussian Blue (PB), a hexacyano-Fe complex material, as a low-cost solid-state cathode in a single-chamber, membrane-free MB.  Microbial oxidn. of org. matter at the anode drives the redn. of PB.  Reduced PB is re-oxidized by exposure to air.  MBs equipped with a PB electrode efficiently transfer charge to PB (85% charge transfer), achieving net energy recovery efficiencies of 18-33%, with no loss of capacity over 20 cycles of operation.  The operating potential for the PB electrode creates a trade-off: increasing the PB electrode potential improves the efficiency of energy recovery, but more material is required to maintain power output and ensure efficient oxidn. of org. matter.  Operation is otherwise straightforward: energy is only required to move reduced PB into the air then back to the MB.  We conclude that PB is a promising cathode material for single-chamber, membrane-free MBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref40"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref40" class="refNumLink">40</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit40" data-doi="10.1021/ja512383b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, J.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, X.-Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Henkelman, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goodenough, J.                         cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Processing Rusty Metals into Versatile Prussian Blue for Sustainable Energy Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Peng, Jian; Zhang, Wang; Wang, Jinsong; Li, Lin; Lai, Weihong; Yang, Qiuran; Zhang, Binwei; Li, Xiaoning; Du, Yumeng; Liu, Hanwen; Wang, Jianli; Cheng, Zhenxiang; Wang, Lizhen; Wang, Shiwen; Wang, Jiazhao; Chou, Shulei; Liu, Huakun; Dou, Shixue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">44</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2102356</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">To reach a closed-loop material system and meet the urgent requirement of sustainable energy storage technologies, it is essential to incorporate efficient waste management into designing new energy storage materials.  Here, a "two birds with one stone" strategy to transform rusty iron products into Prussian blue as high-performance cathode materials, and recover the rusty iron products to their original status, is reported.  Owing to the high cryst. and Na+ content, the rusty iron derived Prussian blue shows a high specific capacity of 145 mAh g-1 and excellent cycling stability over 3500 cycles.  Through the in situ X-ray diffraction and in situ Raman spectra, it is found that the impressive ion storage capability and stability are strongly related to the suppressed structure distortion during the charge/discharge process.  The ion migration mechanism and the possibility to serve as a universal host for other kinds of ions are further illuminated by d. functional theory calcns.  This work provides a new strategy for recycling wasted materials into high value-added materials for sustainable battery systems, and is adaptable in the nanomedicine, catalysis, sensors, and gas storage applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref43" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2020.04.027"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lai, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, Q.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.-K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.-X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Understanding rhombohedral iron hexacyanoferrate with three different sodium positions for high power and long stability sodium-ion battery</span>. <i>Energy Storage Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>30</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">42</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">51</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Continuous Conductive Networks Built by Prussian Blue Cubes and Mesoporous Carbon Lead to Enhanced Sodium-Ion Storage Performances</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Ziheng; Huang, Yongxin; Chu, Ditong; Li, Cheng; Zhang, Yixin; Wu, Feng; Li, Li; Xie, Man; Huang, Jiaqi; Chen, Renjie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">32</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">38202-38212</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The challenges of improving elec. conductivities and enhanced rapid dynamics are active research areas in the modification of Prussian blue (PB) and Prussian blue analogs (PBAs), which are used as excellent cathodes of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  Herein, the terephthalic acid etched stepwise hollow bulky PB cubes and the intimate contact mesoporous carbon (CMK-3) particles with the adhered mini-size PB cubes can together build continuous conductive networks.  The composite (donated as N-PB@CMK) has high elec. cond., low resistance, and ultrahigh sp. surface, which can lead to high capacitive contribution ratios.  The N-PB@CMK electrode can deliver a discharge capacity of 120 mAh g-1 and maintain retention of 85.0% after cycling for 200 cycles at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1.  Even cycling at 1 A g-1, the reversible capacity can be measured to 102 mAh g-1 and exhibit stability over a long cycle.  In situ Raman spectroscopy and X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were further measured to illustrate the phase transition of crystal structure along with the extn./insertion processes of Na+ ions.  Esp., the assembled full cell with NaTi2(PO4)3@C anode can also show good stability and provide promising insights of applying the N-PB@CMK for energy storage systems in the future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref45" class="refNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit45" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-020-14444-4"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, Q.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.-K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.-X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Reversible structural evolution of sodium-rich rhombohedral Prussian blue for sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Nat.                         cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Reversible structural evolution of sodium-rich rhombohedral Prussian blue for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Wanlin; Gang, Yong; Hu, Zhe; Yan, Zichao; Li, Weijie; Li, Yongcheng; Gu, Qin-Fen; Wang, Zhixing; Chou, Shu-Lei; Liu, Hua-Kun; Dou, Shi-Xue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">980</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Research</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Iron-based Prussian blue analogs are promising low-cost and easily prepd. cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries.  Their materials quality and electrochem. performance are heavily reliant on the pptn. process.  Here we report a controllable pptn. method to synthesize high-performance Prussian blue for sodium-ion storage.  Characterization of the nucleation and evolution processes of the highly cryst. Prussian blue microcubes reveals a rhombohedral structure that exhibits high initial Coulombic efficiency, excellent rate performance, and cycling properties.  The phase transitions in the as-obtained material are investigated by synchrotron in situ powder X-ray diffraction, which shows highly reversible structural transformations between rhombohedral, cubic, and tetragonal structures upon sodium-ion (de)intercalations.  Moreover, the Prussian blue material from a large-scale synthesis process shows stable cycling performance in a pouch full cell over 1000 times.  We believe that this work could pave the way for the real application of Prussian blue materials in sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref46"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref46" class="refNumLink">46</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit46" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201702856"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, J.</span></span>;                         cas_cit47R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">47</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Unconventional Mn Vacancies in Mn-Fe Prussian Blue Analogs: Suppressing Jahn-Teller Distortion for Ultrastable Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Shang, Yang; Li, Xiaoxia; Song, Jiajia; Huang, Shaozhuan; Yang, Zhao; Xu, Zhichuan J.; Yang, Hui Ying</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chem</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1804-1818</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Cell Press</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Mn-Fe Prussian blue analogs (PBAs) cathode materials suffer from poor cycling reversibility and capacity retention during phase transition from cubic to tetragonal phase, which is related to the large structural deformation of Mn-N6 octahedra caused by Jahn-Teller distortion.  Herein, we explore a controllable strategy for creating unconventional cation Mn vacancies (VMn) on the surface of Mn-Fe PBAs by utilizing a strong chelating agent-ethylenediaminet etraacetic acid disodium.  The VMn in Mn-Fe PBAs can restrain the movement of Mn-N bonds and thereby mitigate the Jahn-Teller distortion of Mn-N6 octahedra, leading to highly reversible phase transitions of Mn-Fe PBAs as well as an outstanding long-term cycling stability and capacity retention for both the half-cell (72.3% after 2,700 cycles at 0.5 A g-1) and full cell (75.5% after 550 cycles at 0.1 A g-1).  This work opens up a universal avenue to explore the relationship between vacancies and electrochem. performances, even in other materials beyond PBAs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref48" class="refNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit48" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-019-09170-5"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hua, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cortie, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Indris, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.-X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">NASICON-type air-stable and all-climate cathode for sodium-ion batteries with low cost and high-power density</span>. <i>Nat. Commun.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>10</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">1</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">1480</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/s41467-019-09170-5</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D10%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D1480%26journal%3DNat.%2BCommun.%26issue%3D1%26author%3DM.%2BChen%26author%3DW.%2BHua%26author%3DJ.%2BXiao%26author%3DD.%2BCortie%26author%3DW.%2BChen%26author%3DE.%2BWang%26author%3DZ.%2BHu%26author%3DQ.%2BGu%26author%3DX.%2BWang%26author%3DS.%2BIndris%26author%3DS.-L.%2BChou%26author%3DS.-X.%2BDou%26title%3DNASICON-type%2Bair-stable%2Band%2Ball-climate%2Bcathode%2Bfor%2Bsodium-ion%2Bbatteries%2Bwith%2Blow%2Bcost%2Band%2Bhigh-power%2Bdensity%26doi%3D10.1038%252Fs41467-019-09170-5&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.1c04492&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09170-5&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">NASICON-type air-stable and all-climate cathode for sodium-ion batteries with low cost and high-power density</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen Mingzhe; Cortie David; Wang Enhui; Hu Zhe; Wang Xiaolin; Chou Shu-Lei; Dou Shi-Xue; Hua Weibo; Indris Sylvio; Xiao Jin; Xiao Jin; Chen Weihua; Wang Enhui; Gu Qinfen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1480</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">The development of low-cost and long-lasting all-climate cathode materials for the sodium ion battery has been one of the key issues for the success of large-scale energy storage.  One option is the utilization of earth-abundant elements such as iron.  Here, we synthesize a NASICON-type tuneable Na4Fe3(PO4)2(P2O7)/C nanocomposite which shows both excellent rate performance and outstanding cycling stability over more than 4400 cycles.  Its air stability and all-climate properties are investigated, and its potential as the sodium host in full cells has been studied.  A remarkably low volume change of 4.0% is observed.  Its high sodium diffusion coefficient has been measured and analysed via first-principles calculations, and its three-dimensional sodium ion diffusion pathways are identified.  Our results indicate that this low-cost and environmentally friendly Na4Fe3(PO4)2(P2O7)/C nanocomposite could be a competitive candidate material for sodium ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref49" class="refNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit49" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201916301"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, J.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.-D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zeng, X.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, Y.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xin, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.-P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.-W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, J.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wan, L.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Enabling a Durable Electrochemical Interface via an Artificial Amorphous Cathode Electrolyte Interphase for Hybrid Solid/Liquid Lithium-Metal Batteries</span>. <i>Angew.                 10.1002/anie.201916301</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D59%26publication_year%3D2020%26pages%3D6585-6589%26journal%3DAngew.%2BChem.%252C%2BInt.%2BEd.%26issue%3D16%26author%3DJ.-Y.%2BLiang%26author%3DX.-D.%2BZhang%26author%3DX.-X.%2BZeng%26author%3DM.%2BYan%26author%3DY.-X.%2BYin%26author%3DS.%2BXin%26author%3DW.-P.%2BWang%26author%3DX.-W.%2BWu%26author%3DJ.-L.%2BShi%26author%3DL.-J.%2BWan%26author%3DY.-G.%2BGuo%26title%3DEnabling%2Ba%2BDurable%2BElectrochemical%2BInterface%2Bvia%2Ban%2BArtificial%2BAmorphous%2BCathode%2BElectrolyte%2BInterphase%2Bfor%2BHybrid%2BSolid%252FLiquid%2BLithium-Metal%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fanie.201916301&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.1c04492&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fanie.201916301&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enabling a Durable Electrochemical Interface via an Artificial Amorphous Cathode Electrolyte Interphase for Hybrid Solid/Liquid Lithium-Metal Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liang, Jia-Yan; Zhang, Xu-Dong; Zeng, Xian-Xiang; Yan, Min; Yin, Ya-Xia; Xin, Sen; Wang, Wen-Peng; Wu, Xiong-Wei; Shi, Ji-Lei; Wan, Li-Jun; Guo, Yu-Guo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">59</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">16</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6585-6589</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACIEF5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1433-7851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A hybrid solid/liq. electrolyte with superior security facilitates the implementation of high-energy-d. storage devices, but it suffers from inferior chem. compatibility with cathodes.  Herein, an optimal lithium difluoro(oxalato)borate salt was introduced to build in situ an amorphous cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) between Ni-rich cathodes and hybrid electrolyte.  The CEI preserves the surface structure with high compatibility, leading to enhanced interfacial stability.  Meanwhile, the space-charge layer can be prominently mitigated at the solid/solid interface via harmonized chem. potentials, acquiring promoted interfacial dynamics as revealed by COMSOL simulation.  Consequently, the amorphous CEI integrates the bifunctionality to provide an excellent cycling stability, high Coulombic efficiency, and favorable rate capability in high-voltage Li-metal batteries, innovating the design philosophy of functional CEI strategy for future high-energy-d. batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref50" class="refNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit50" data-doi="10.1039/C3EE44004D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">You, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, Y.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-quality Prussian blue crystals as superior cathode materials for room-temperature sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Energy Environ.                 10.1039/C3EE44004D</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D7%26publication_year%3D2014%26pages%3D1643-1647%26journal%3DEnergy%2BEnviron.%2BSci.%26issue%3D5%26author%3DY.%2BYou%26author%3DX.-L.%2BWu%26author%3DY.-X.%2BYin%26author%3DY.-G.%2BGuo%26title%3DHigh-quality%2BPrussian%2Bblue%2Bcrystals%2Bas%2Bsuperior%2Bcathode%2Bmaterials%2Bfor%2Broom-temperature%2Bsodium-ion%2Bbatteries%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC3EE44004D&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.1c04492&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC3EE44004D&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit50R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">50</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-quality Prussian blue crystals as superior cathode materials for room-temperature sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">You, Ya; Wu, Xing-Long; Yin, Ya-Xia; Guo, Yu-Guo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1643-1647</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-quality Prussian blue crystals with a small no. of vacancies and a low water content are obtained by employing Na4Fe(CN)6 as the single iron-source precursor.  The high-quality Prussian blue shows high specific capacity and remarkable cycling stability as the cathode material for Na-ion batteries because of its excellent ion storage capability and impressive structure stability.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref51" class="refNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit51" data-doi="10.1002/inf2.12023"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent research progresses in ether- and ester-based electrolytes for sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>InfoMat</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>1</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">3</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">376</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">389</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit51R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">51</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent research progresses in ether- and ester-based electrolytes for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lin, Zeheng; Xia, Qingbing; Wang, Wanlin; Li, Weishan; Chou, Shulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">InfoMat</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">376-389</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">INFOHH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2567-3165</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">John Wiley &amp; Sons Australia, Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to the natural abundance and low cost of sodium resources, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have drawn considerable attention for state-of-the-art power storage devices over the last few years.  To enable advanced SIBs with a brighter future, great effort has been made, not only through optimizing the electrode materials, but also with rationally designing various electrolyte systems.  Among the available electrolyte systems, org. electrolytes, esp. those based on esters as well as ethers, are the most promising ones for practical application in the foreseeable future, due to their numerous inherent advantages.  This review is concerned with the recent research progresses on org. electrolytes for SIBs, focusing on ether-based and ester-based ones.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187eb4f853075b-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34'],'ref35':['cit35'],'ref36':['cit36'],'ref37':['cit37'],'ref38':['cit38'],'ref39':['cit39'],'ref40':['cit40'],'ref41':['cit41'],'ref42':['cit42'],'ref43':['cit43'],'ref44':['cit44'],'ref45':['cit45'],'ref46':['cit46'],'ref47':['cit47'],'ref48':['cit48'],'ref49':['cit49'],'ref50':['cit50'],'ref51':['cit51']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

(f) Rate performances of W-PB and I-PB at current densities from 30 to 1500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (g, h) Cycling performances of W-PB and I-PB at the current densities of 150 and 750 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/large/nl1c04492_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig3"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/medium/nl1c04492_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c04492/asset/images/large/nl1c04492_0003.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr3" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>4</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">7</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">612</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">621</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        rightTab-cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Estimation of global final-stage energy-return-on-investment for fossil fuels with comparison to renewable energy sources</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Brockway, Paul E.; Owen, Anne; Brand-Correa, Lina I.; Hardt, Lukas</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">612-621</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2058-7546</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Research</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Under many scenarios, fossil fuels are projected to remain the dominant energy source until at least 2050.  However, harder-to-reach fossil fuels require more energy to ext. and, hence, are coming at an increasing 'energy cost'.  Assocd. declines in fossil fuel energy-return-on-investment ratios at first appear of little concern, given that published ests. for oil, coal and gas are typically above 25:1.  However, such ratios are measured at the primary energy stage and should instead be estd. at the final stage where energy enters the economy (for example, electricity and petrol).  Here, we calc. global time series (1995-2011) energy-return-on-investment ratios for fossil fuels at both primary and final energy stages.  We concur with common primary-stage ests. (∼30:1), but find very low ratios at the final stage: around 6:1 and declining.  This implies that fossil fuel energy-return-on-investment ratios may be much closer to those of renewables than previously expected and that they could decline precipitously in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1038/451652a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Armand, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J.-M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Building better batteries</span>. <i>Nature</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2008</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>451</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">7179</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">652</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">657</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202100757"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hua, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lai, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, W.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Architecting Amorphous Vanadium Oxide/MXene Nanohybrid via Tunable Anodic Oxidation for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The exploration of next-generation sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) is a worldwide concern to replace the current com. lithium-ion batteries, mitigating the increasing exhaustion of Li resources.  Sodium transition metal oxides are considered to be one of the most promising cathode materials for SIBs.  The anionic redox reaction in Li-rich transition metal oxides is capable of providing extra capacity in addn. to the cationic redox activities in lithium-ion batteries.  A similar phenomenon exists in SIBs, which even applies to Na-deficient transition metal oxides.  Moreover, transition metal oxides with mixed phase also demonstrate great potential.  In this review, studies on anionic redox are first systematically introduced.  The up-to-date advances on high-capacity transition metal oxide cathode materials for SIBs are then classified and summarized in different groups assocd. with or without anionic redox.  The existing challenges as well as available solns. and strategies are discussed, and proposals with new insights are made at the end.  It is expected that this work can provide new perspectives on controlling the anionic redox activity and finding novel high-capacity oxide cathode materials for SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref11" class="rightTabRefNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit11" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-021-22523-3"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, G.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, Y.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, R.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Tuning local chemistry of P2 layered-oxide cathode for high energy and long cycles of sodium-ion battery</span>. <i>Nat.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na3V2(PO4)2F3 is a promising cathode candidate for sodium ion batteries due to its high working voltage.  However, an "unknown" low-voltage plateau around ∼3.3 V lowers its operating voltage and puzzles researchers.  Herein, the reasons for its appearance are explored.  It is found that the formation of the low-voltage plateau originates from the extra-generated [VO6] induced by the fluorine loss during the synthesis.         </div><div class="casAbstract">As a kind of high-voltage cathode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), the instability of a cathode-electrolyte interface (CEI) of Prussian blue (PB) results in the consumption of electrolyte and poor cycling life.  Herein, an artificial NaF-rich CEI is in-situ designed on a PB cathode via a chem. presodiated strategy to remarkably prolong the cycling life of SIBs.  Based on a spontaneous redox reaction between a chem. presodiation solvent and a polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) binder, a homogeneous, thin, and NaF-rich CEI is conformally coated on the surface of PB, forming CEI@PB.  The components of CEI within CEI@PB mainly comprise inorg. Na-salts (NaF, Na2CO3, and NaHCO3) and org. Na-based species (R-O-Na, R-CO-Na, and R-O2CO-Na), which can ensure the rapid transport of Na+ ions while effectively preventing CEI@PB against the attack of org. solvent and improving its cycling stability.  Besides, d. functional theory calcns. also reveal that NaF is a good electronic insulator and ionic conductor, which is suitable as a stable CEI.  Benefiting from the aforementioned merits, the CEI@PB cathode with a high areal capacity of 0.61 mAh cm-2 delivers an ultra-long cycling life of 3000 cycles.  More importantly, the concept of in-situ CEI can be extended to other cathodes beyond Na batteries discussed herein.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref19" class="rightTabRefNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit19" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.202108616"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, X.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lai, W.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.-L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Advanced Characterization Techniques Paving the Way for Commercialization of Low-Cost Prussian Blue Analog Cathodes</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review Aq. energy devices are under the spotlight of current research due to their safety, low cost and ease of handling.  Metal-org. frameworks (MOFs) and their derivs. have spurred extensive exploration as they provide a library of new electrode materials.  The rich and structural flexibilities (such as metal nodes, ligands, pore structure) endow MOFs and MOFs-derivs. with vast opportunities for various energy devices.  In this review, we discuss the correlation between MOF structural parameters and electrochem. performance for aq. energy devices in the scope of zinc-based batteries (Zn-ion, Zn-alk. and Zn-air batteries), potassium-ion batteries and supercapacitors.  For each energy device, the effect of determinative factors and structural modulating strategies of MOFs and derivs. are highlighted. Here we report high-quality NaxFeFe(CN)6 nanocubes as cathode materials for SIBs and investigate their sodium storage mechanism.  Among them, Na-rich Na1.70FeFe(CN)6 shows highly reversible electrochem. reactions, delivering a capacity as high as 120.7 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 200 mA g-1; even at 1200 mA g-1, the capacity still retains up to 73.6 mA h g-1.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The design of Faradaic battery electrodes that exhibit high rate capability and long cycle life equiv. to those of the electrodes of elec. double-layer capacitors is a big challenge.  Here we report a strategy to fill this performance gap using the concept of Grotthuss proton conduction, in which proton transfer takes place by means of concerted cleavage and formation of O-H bonds in a hydrogen-bonding network.  We show that in a hydrated Prussian blue analog (Turnbull's blue) the abundant lattice water mols. with a contiguous hydrogen-bonding network facilitate Grotthuss proton conduction during redox reactions.  When using it as a battery electrode, we find high-rate behaviors at 4,000 C (380 A g-1, 508 mA cm-2), and a long cycling life of 0.73 million cycles.  These results for diffusion-free Grotthuss topochem. of protons, in contrast to orthodox battery electrochem., which requires ion diffusion inside electrodes, indicate a potential direction to revolutionize electrochem. energy storage for high-power applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref38" class="rightTabRefNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit38" data-doi="10.3390/cryst11080895"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Morozova, P.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The Prussian Blue analog K2-δMn[Fe(CN)6]1-γnH2O is regarded as a key candidate for potassium-ion battery pos. electrode materials due to its high specific capacity and redox potential, easy scalability, and low cost.  However, various intrinsic defects, such as water in the crystal lattice, can drastically affect electrochem. performance.  In this work, we varied the water content in K2-δMn[Fe(CN)6]1-γnH2O by using a vacuum/air drying procedure and investigated its effect on the crystal structure, chem. compn. and electrochem. properties.  The crystal structure of K2-δMn[Fe(CN)6]1-γnH2O was, for the first time, Rietveld-refined, based on neutron powder diffraction data at 10 and 300 K, suggesting a new structural model with the Pc space group in accordance with Mossbauer spectroscopy. Nanosized cathode materials delivered electrochem. specific capacities of 130-134 mAh g-1 at 30 mA g-1 (C/5) in the 2.5-4.5 V (vs.K+/K) potential range.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Microbial fuel cells can directly convert chem. energy into elec. energy, but significant energy losses result from the use of O2 as the cathode.  Microbial batteries (MBs) replace conventional O2 cathodes with solid-state cathodes that can be re-oxidized under favorable conditions, enabling more efficient energy recovery.  Previously, we demonstrated proof-of-concept for MBs with a silver oxide cathode.  The high cost of silver and energy required for re-oxidn. make this material impractical.  In this work, we evaluate Prussian Blue (PB), a hexacyano-Fe complex material, as a low-cost solid-state cathode in a single-chamber, membrane-free MB.  Microbial oxidn. of org. matter at the anode drives the redn. of PB.  Reduced PB is re-oxidized by exposure to air.  MBs equipped with a PB electrode efficiently transfer charge to PB (85% charge transfer), achieving net energy recovery efficiencies of 18-33%, with no loss of capacity over 20 cycles of operation.  The operating potential for the PB electrode creates a trade-off: increasing the PB electrode potential improves the efficiency of energy recovery, but more material is required to maintain power output and ensure efficient oxidn. of org. matter.  Operation is otherwise straightforward: energy is only required to move reduced PB into the air then back to the MB.  We conclude that PB is a promising cathode material for single-chamber, membrane-free MBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref40"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref40" class="rightTabRefNumLink">40</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit40" data-doi="10.1021/ja512383b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, J.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, X.-Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Henkelman, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goodenough, J.
